All the topics in this book are covered in more detail in S. J. Blackmore, [3mConsciousness: An Introduction [0m(London: Hodder & 
Stoughton; New York: Oxford University Press, 2003), along with exercises, demonstrations, and an extensive list of references. 

D. C. Dennett, [3mConsciousness Explained [0m(Boston, MA, and London: Little, Brown and Co., 1991) provides a deep and fascinating 
philosophical approach. For opposing views, see D. Chalmers, [3mThe Conscious Mind [0m(Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1996) and J. 
Searle, [3mThe Mystery of Consciousness [0m(London and New York: Granta Books, 1998). 

For psychology and neuroscience, try F. Crick, [3mThe Astonishing Hypothesis [0m(New York: Scribnerโs, 1994) (a strong reductionist 
view), and G. M. Edelman and G. Tononi, [3mConsciousness: How Matter Becomes Imagination [0m(London: Penguin, 2000). (In the USA, this 
is published as [3mA Universe of Consciousness: How Matter Becomes Imagination [0m, by Basic Books.) A. Zeman, [3mConsciousness: A Userโs 
Guide [0m(New Haven, CT: Yale University Press, 2002) is a good overview. 

William Jamesโs two-volume classic is [3mThe Principles of Psychology [0m(London: MacMillan, 1890), and for some fun reading try D. R. 
Hofstadter and D. C. Dennett (eds), [3mThe Mindโs I: Fantasies and Reflections on Self and Soul [0m(London: Penguin, 1981).
